	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Western canon</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Parnaso_09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Parnaso_09.jpg/200px-Parnaso_09.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Parnaso_09.jpg/300px-Parnaso_09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Parnaso_09.jpg/400px-Parnaso_09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="635" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Parnaso_09.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dante" class="mw-redirect" title="Dante">Dante</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> in <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Sanzio" class="mw-redirect" title="Raphael Sanzio">Raphael</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Parnassus" title="The Parnassus">Parnassus</a></i> fresco (1511), key figures in the Western canon</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg/220px-Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg/330px-Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg/440px-Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="917" data-file-height="1146" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cropped_image_of_Sappho_from_Raphael%27s_Parnassus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of Sappho from <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Parnassus" title="The Parnassus">Parnassus</a></i> (1510-11), shown alongside other poets. In her left hand, she holds a scroll with her name written on it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohannides198320_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohannides198320-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pablo_Picasso,_1910,_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_(Fanny_Tellier),_oil_on_canvas,_100.3_x_73.6_cm,_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Pablo_Picasso%2C_1910%2C_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_%28Fanny_Tellier%29%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_100.3_x_73.6_cm%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg/220px-Pablo_Picasso%2C_1910%2C_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_%28Fanny_Tellier%29%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_100.3_x_73.6_cm%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="304" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Pablo_Picasso%2C_1910%2C_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_%28Fanny_Tellier%29%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_100.3_x_73.6_cm%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg/330px-Pablo_Picasso%2C_1910%2C_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_%28Fanny_Tellier%29%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_100.3_x_73.6_cm%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Pablo_Picasso%2C_1910%2C_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_%28Fanny_Tellier%29%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_100.3_x_73.6_cm%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg/440px-Pablo_Picasso%2C_1910%2C_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_%28Fanny_Tellier%29%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_100.3_x_73.6_cm%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1770" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pablo_Picasso,_1910,_Girl_with_a_Mandolin_(Fanny_Tellier),_oil_on_canvas,_100.3_x_73.6_cm,_Museum_of_Modern_Art_New_York..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Picasso" class="mw-redirect" title="Picasso">Picasso</a>, <i>Girl with a Mandolin (Fanny Tellier)</i> (1910), oil on canvas, 100.3 × 73.6&#160;cm, <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Modern_Art" title="Museum of Modern Art">Museum of Modern Art</a>, New York</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Western canon</b> is the body of <a href="/wiki/High_culture" title="High culture">high culture</a> literature, music, philosophy, and works of art that is highly valued in the <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">West</a>: works that have achieved the status of <a href="/wiki/Classic" title="Classic">classics</a>. However, not all these works originate in the Western world, and such works are also valued throughout the world. It is "a certain Western intellectual tradition that goes from, say, <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wittgenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Wittgenstein">Wittgenstein</a> in philosophy, and from <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> to <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a> in literature".<sup id="cite_ref-Searle_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Searle-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The word <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/canon" class="extiw" title="wikt:canon">canon</a> is derived from ancient Greek <i>κανών, kanṓn</i>, meaning a measuring rod, or standard. The <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, a product of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Jewish">ancient Jewish</a> culture, from the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, has been a major force in shaping <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>, and "has inspired some of the great monuments of human thought, literature, and art".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The canon of books has been fairly stable, although it has expanded to include more women and racial minorities, while the canons of music and the visual arts have greatly expanded to cover the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, and subsequent centuries once largely overlooked. But some examples of newer media such as <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">cinema</a> have attained a precarious position in the canon. Also during the twentieth century there has been a growing interest in the West, as well as globally, in major artistic works of the cultures of Asia, Africa, the Middle East, and South America, including the former colonies of European nations.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="A_classic">A classic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: A classic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa,_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci,_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/220px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/330px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg/440px-Mona_Lisa%2C_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci%2C_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7479" data-file-height="11146" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mona_Lisa,_by_Leonardo_da_Vinci,_from_C2RMF_retouched.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In painting, the <i><a href="/wiki/Mona_Lisa" title="Mona Lisa">Mona Lisa</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a>, c. 1503–1506, is the archetypal masterpiece, though it was not produced for admission to a guild or academy.</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Classic" title="Classic">classic</a> is a book, or any other work of art, accepted as being exemplary or noteworthy, for example through an <a href="/wiki/Imprimatur" title="Imprimatur">imprimatur</a> such as being listed in a list of <a href="/wiki/Great_books" title="Great books">great books</a>, or through a reader's personal opinion. Although the term is often associated with the Western canon, it can be applied to works of literature, music and art, etc. from all traditions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">Chinese classics</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Veda" class="mw-redirect" title="Veda">Vedas</a>. A related word is <a href="/wiki/Masterpiece" title="Masterpiece">masterpiece</a> or <i>chef d'œuvre</i>, which in modern use refers to a creation that has been given much critical praise, especially one that is considered the greatest work of a person's career or to a work of outstanding creativity, skill, or workmanship. Historically, the word refers to a work of a very high standard produced in order to obtain membership of a <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">Guild</a> or <a href="/wiki/Academy" title="Academy">Academy</a>.
</p><p>The first writer to use the term "classic" was <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Gellius" title="Aulus Gellius">Aulus Gellius</a>, a 2nd-century <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> writer who, in the <a href="/wiki/Miscellany" title="Miscellany">miscellany</a> <i>Noctes Atticae</i> (19, 8, 15), refers to a writer as a <i>classicus scriptor, non proletarius</i> ("A distinguished, not a commonplace writer"). Such classification began with the Greeks' <i>ranking</i> their cultural works, with the word <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/canon" class="extiw" title="wikt:canon">canon</a></i> (ancient Greek κανών, kanṓn: "measuring rod, standard"). Moreover, early <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> Church Fathers used <i>canon</i> to rank the authoritative texts of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a>, preserving them, given the expense of <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> and mechanical book reproduction, thus, being comprehended in a <i>canon</i> ensured a book's preservation as the best way to retain information about a civilization.<sup id="cite_ref-Gellius_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gellius-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Contemporarily, the Western canon defines the best of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>. In the ancient world, at the <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_Library" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexandrian Library">Alexandrian Library</a>, scholars coined the Greek term <i>Hoi enkrithentes</i> ("the admitted", "the included") to identify the writers in the canon.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_canon">Literary canon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Literary canon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classic_book">Classic book</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classic book">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Classic_book" title="Classic book">Classic book</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_books" title="Great books">Great books</a></div>
<p>With regard to books, what makes a book "classic" has concerned various authors, from <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a> to <a href="/wiki/Italo_Calvino" title="Italo Calvino">Italo Calvino</a>, and questions such as "Why Read the Classics?", and "What Is a Classic?" have been considered by others, including Calvino, <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Augustin_Sainte-Beuve" title="Charles Augustin Sainte-Beuve">Charles Augustin Sainte-Beuve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dirda" title="Michael Dirda">Michael Dirda</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>.
</p><p>The terms "classic book" and Western canon are closely related concepts, but are not necessarily synonymous. A "canon" is a list of books considered to be "essential", and it can be published as a collection (such as <a href="/wiki/Great_Books_of_the_Western_World" title="Great Books of the Western World">Great Books of the Western World</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Library" title="Modern Library">Modern Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Everyman%27s_Library" title="Everyman&#39;s Library">Everyman's Library</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Penguin_Classics" title="Penguin Classics">Penguin Classics</a>), presented as a list with an academic's imprimatur (such as <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bloom" title="Harold Bloom">Harold Bloom</a>'s,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>) or be the official reading list of a university. 
</p><p>Some of the writers who are generally considered the most important in <a href="/wiki/Western_literature" title="Western literature">Western literature</a> are  <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aeschylus" title="Aeschylus">Aeschylus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Rabelais" title="François Rabelais">François Rabelais</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Michel de Montaigne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">Miguel de Cervantes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lope_de_Vega" title="Lope de Vega">Lope de Vega</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">Molière</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Racine" title="Jean Racine">Jean Racine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlo_Goldoni" title="Carlo Goldoni">Carlo Goldoni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Johann Wolfgang von Goethe</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stendhal" title="Stendhal">Stendhal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_de_Balzac" title="Honoré de Balzac">Honoré de Balzac</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pushkin" title="Alexander Pushkin">Alexander Pushkin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Gogol" title="Nikolai Gogol">Nikolai Gogol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Turgenev" title="Ivan Turgenev">Ivan Turgenev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman" title="Walt Whitman">Walt Whitman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Baudelaire" title="Charles Baudelaire">Charles Baudelaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">Gustave Flaubert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Ibsen" title="Henrik Ibsen">Henrik Ibsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emily_Dickinson" title="Emily Dickinson">Emily Dickinson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Rimbaud" title="Arthur Rimbaud">Arthur Rimbaud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anton_Chekhov" title="Anton Chekhov">Anton Chekhov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Proust" title="Marcel Proust">Marcel Proust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Franz Kafka</a>, <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">William Faulkner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bulgakov" title="Mikhail Bulgakov">Mikhail Bulgakov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Neruda" title="Pablo Neruda">Pablo Neruda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Pessoa" title="Fernando Pessoa">Fernando Pessoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Camus" title="Albert Camus">Albert Camus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Beckett" title="Samuel Beckett">Samuel Beckett</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In addition the following are some of the important works from other cultures that have influenced the West: <i><a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh" title="Gilgamesh">Gilgamesh</a></i> (c. 2100 BC), <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i> (c. 800 BC), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="The Bible">The Bible</a></i> (c. 5th century BC - 1st century AD), <i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a></i> (c. 7th century AD), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Murasaki_Shikibu" title="Murasaki Shikibu">Murasaki Shikibu</a> (c. 973 or 978 – c. 1014 or 1031), and <i><a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Red_Chamber" title="Dream of the Red Chamber">Dream of the Red Chamber</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Cao_Xueqin" title="Cao Xueqin">Cao Xueqin</a> (1715 or 1724 – 1763 or 1764).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> 
<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Bloom.27s_four_ages"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bloom's_four_ages">Bloom's four ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Bloom&#039;s four ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The American literary critic <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bloom" title="Harold Bloom">Harold Bloom</a> has divided the body of Western Literature into four ages:<sup id="cite_ref-auto_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Theocratic_Age">The Theocratic Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The Theocratic Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>(2000 BC – 1321 AD), with five main traditions that influenced the West:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Snakesacrifice.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Snakesacrifice.jpg/220px-Snakesacrifice.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Snakesacrifice.jpg/330px-Snakesacrifice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Snakesacrifice.jpg/440px-Snakesacrifice.jpg 2x" data-file-width="453" data-file-height="627" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Snakesacrifice.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The snake sacrifice of Janamejaya. <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i></div></div></div>
<ul><li>The Ancient Near East; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Gilgamesh" title="Gilgamesh">Gilgamesh</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_the_Dead" class="mw-redirect" title="The Book of the Dead">The Book of the Dead</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a></i></li>
<li>Ancient India; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a></i></li>
<li>Ancient Greece; e.g. the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Oedipus_Rex" title="Oedipus Rex">Oedipus Rex</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a></li>
<li>Ancient Rome; e.g. the <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses_(Ovid)" class="mw-redirect" title="Metamorphoses (Ovid)">Metamorphoses</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>)</li>
<li>The Middle Ages; e.g. the <i><a href="/wiki/Confessions_(Augustine)" title="Confessions (Augustine)">Confessions</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Augustine" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Augustine">Saint Augustine</a></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Aristocratic_Age">The Aristocratic Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Aristocratic Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>(1321–1832), with five major bodies of literature:
</p>
<ul><li>Italy; e.g. the <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Dante" class="mw-redirect" title="Dante">Dante</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince" title="The Prince">The Prince</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Machiavelli" class="mw-redirect" title="Machiavelli">Machiavelli</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Servant_of_Two_Masters" title="The Servant of Two Masters">The Servant of Two Masters</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Carlo_Goldoni" title="Carlo Goldoni">Carlo Goldoni</a>)</li>
<li>France; e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Essays_(Montaigne)" title="Essays (Montaigne)">Essays</a> (<a href="/wiki/Montaigne" class="mw-redirect" title="Montaigne">Montaigne</a>), <a href="/wiki/The_Misanthrope" title="The Misanthrope">The Misanthrope</a> (<a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">Molière</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Candide" title="Candide">Candide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>)</li>
<li>Germany; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Robbers" title="The Robbers">The Robbers</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Schiller" title="Friedrich Schiller">Friedrich Schiller</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Faust" title="Faust">Faust</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Italian_Journey" title="Italian Journey">Italian Journey</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>)</li>
<li>Spain and Portugal; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">Don Quixote</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Miguel de Cervantes">Miguel de Cervantes</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Trickster_of_Seville" class="mw-redirect" title="The Trickster of Seville">The Trickster of Seville</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Tirso_de_Molina" title="Tirso de Molina">Tirso de Molina</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lusiads" class="mw-redirect" title="The Lusiads">The Lusiads</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Camoens" class="mw-redirect" title="Luis de Camoens">Luis de Camoens</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Ireland</a>; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost" title="Paradise Lost">Paradise Lost</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Gulliver%27s_Travels" title="Gulliver&#39;s Travels">Gulliver's Travels</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Swift" title="Jonathan Swift">Jonathan Swift</a>)</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Democratic_Age">The Democratic Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: The Democratic Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>(1832–1900), when the strength of American and Russian literature begins
</p>
<ul><li>United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Pride_and_Prejudice" title="Pride and Prejudice">Pride and Prejudice</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Oliver_Twist" class="mw-redirect" title="The Adventures of Oliver Twist">The Adventures of Oliver Twist</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Picture_of_Dorian_Gray" title="The Picture of Dorian Gray">The Picture of Dorian Gray</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde">Oscar Wilde</a>)</li>
<li>Italy; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Betrothed_(Manzoni_novel)" title="The Betrothed (Manzoni novel)">The Betrothed</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Manzoni" title="Alessandro Manzoni">Alessandro Manzoni</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Pinocchio" title="The Adventures of Pinocchio">The Adventures of Pinocchio</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Carlo_Collodi" title="Carlo Collodi">Carlo Collodi</a>)</li>
<li>France; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Red_and_the_Black" title="The Red and the Black">The Red and the Black</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Stendhal" title="Stendhal">Stendhal</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Madame_Bovary" title="Madame Bovary">Madame Bovary</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">Gustave Flaubert</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Mis%C3%A9rables" title="Les Misérables">Les Misérables</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>)</li>
<li>Germany; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ring_of_the_Nibelung" class="mw-redirect" title="The Ring of the Nibelung">The Ring of the Nibelung</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Richard_Wagner" title="Richard Wagner">Richard Wagner</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Children%27s_and_Household_Tales" class="mw-redirect" title="Children&#39;s and Household Tales">Children's and Household Tales</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Grimm_Brothers" class="mw-redirect" title="Grimm Brothers">Grimm Brothers</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Effi_Briest" title="Effi Briest">Effi Briest</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Theodor_Fontane" title="Theodor Fontane">Theodor Fontane</a>)</li>
<li>Spain and Portugal; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Fortunata_and_Jacinta" class="mw-redirect" title="Fortunata and Jacinta">Fortunata and Jacinta</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Benito_P%C3%A9rez_Gald%C3%B3s" title="Benito Pérez Galdós">Benito Pérez Galdós</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/La_Regenta" class="mw-redirect" title="La Regenta">La Regenta</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Leopoldo_Alas" title="Leopoldo Alas">Leopoldo Alas</a>)</li>
<li>Russia; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Crime_and_Punishment" title="Crime and Punishment">Crime and Punishment</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Dostoevsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Dostoevsky">Dostoevsky</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/War_and_Peace" title="War and Peace">War and Peace</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Seagull" title="The Seagull">The Seagull</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Anton_Chekhov" title="Anton Chekhov">Anton Chekhov</a>)</li>
<li>United States; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" class="mw-redirect" title="The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn">The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">Mark Twain</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Moby-Dick" title="Moby-Dick">Moby-Dick</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>)</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg/170px-Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg/255px-Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg/340px-Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="548" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Famous_fantastic_mysteries_195306.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kafka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kafka">Kafka</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Metamorphosis" title="The Metamorphosis">The Metamorphosis</a></i> was even reprinted in the June 1953 issue of the pulp magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Famous_Fantastic_Mysteries" title="Famous Fantastic Mysteries">Famous Fantastic Mysteries</a></i></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Chaotic_Age">The Chaotic Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The Chaotic Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>(1900–today), which includes a multitude of countries and authors:
</p>
<ul><li>United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(novel)" title="Ulysses (novel)"> Ulysses</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">James Joyce</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Mrs_Dalloway" title="Mrs Dalloway">Mrs Dalloway</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a>)' and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Waste_Land" title="The Waste Land">The Waste Land</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/T.S._Eliot" class="mw-redirect" title="T.S. Eliot">T.S. Eliot</a>)</li>
<li>Italy; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Six_Characters_in_Search_of_an_Author" title="Six Characters in Search of an Author">Six Characters in Search of an Author</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Luigi_Pirandello" title="Luigi Pirandello">Luigi Pirandello</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Zeno%27s_Conscience" title="Zeno&#39;s Conscience">Zeno's Conscience</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Italo_Svevo" title="Italo Svevo">Italo Svevo</a>)</li>
<li>France; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/In_Search_of_Lost_Time" title="In Search of Lost Time">In Search of Lost Time</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Marcel_Proust" title="Marcel Proust">Marcel Proust</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stranger_(Camus_novel)" title="The Stranger (Camus novel)"> The Stranger</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Albert_Camus" title="Albert Camus">Albert Camus</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Waiting_for_Godot" title="Waiting for Godot">Waiting for Godot</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Samuel_Beckett" title="Samuel Beckett">Samuel Beckett</a>)</li>
<li>Germany and German-speaking Central Europe; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Magic_Mountain" title="The Magic Mountain">The Magic Mountain</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Castle_(novel)" title="The Castle (novel)"> The Castle </a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Franz Kafka</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_Without_Qualities" title="The Man Without Qualities">The Man Without Qualities</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Robert_Musil" title="Robert Musil">Robert Musil</a>)
<ul><li>Spain and Portugal; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Gypsy_Ballads" title="Gypsy Ballads">Gypsy Ballads</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Federico_Garcia_Lorca" class="mw-redirect" title="Federico Garcia Lorca">Federico Garcia Lorca</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Disquiet" title="The Book of Disquiet">The Book of Disquiet</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Fernando_Pessoa" title="Fernando Pessoa">Fernando Pessoa</a>)</li></ul></li>
<li>Russia; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Master_and_Margarita" title="The Master and Margarita">The Master and Margarita</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Boulgakov" class="mw-redirect" title="Mikhail Boulgakov">Mikhail Boulgakov</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gulag_Archipelago" title="The Gulag Archipelago">The Gulag Archipelago</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn" title="Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn">Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</a>)</li>
<li>United States; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Gatsby" title="The Great Gatsby">The Great Gatsby</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/F._Scott_Fitzgerald" title="F. Scott Fitzgerald">F. Scott Fitzgerald</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Old_Man_and_the_Sea" title="The Old Man and the Sea">The Old Man and the Sea</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>)</li>
<li>Latin America; e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Ficciones" title="Ficciones">Ficciones</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">Jorge Luis Borges</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Canto_General" title="Canto General">Canto General</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Pablo_Neruda" title="Pablo Neruda">Pablo Neruda</a>)  and <i><a href="/wiki/One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Garcia_Marquez" class="mw-redirect" title="Gabriel Garcia Marquez">Gabriel Garcia Marquez</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-auto_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Books_Program">Great Books Program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Great Books Program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Books.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Great_Books.jpg/220px-Great_Books.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Great_Books.jpg/330px-Great_Books.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Great_Books.jpg/440px-Great_Books.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Books.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Books_of_the_Western_World" title="Great Books of the Western World">Great Books of the Western World</a></i> in 60 volumes</div></div></div>
<p>A university or college <a href="/wiki/Great_Books" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Books">Great Books</a> Program is a program inspired by the Great Books movement begun in the United States in the 1920s by Prof. <a href="/wiki/John_Erskine_(educator)" title="John Erskine (educator)">John Erskine</a> of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, which proposed to improve the higher education system by returning it to the western <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal arts">liberal arts</a> tradition of broad cross-disciplinary learning. These academics and educators included <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hutchins" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Hutchins">Robert Hutchins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mortimer_Adler" class="mw-redirect" title="Mortimer Adler">Mortimer Adler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stringfellow_Barr" title="Stringfellow Barr">Stringfellow Barr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Buchanan" title="Scott Buchanan">Scott Buchanan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Barzun" title="Jacques Barzun">Jacques Barzun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Meiklejohn" title="Alexander Meiklejohn">Alexander Meiklejohn</a>. The view among them was that the emphasis on narrow specialization in American colleges had harmed the quality of <a href="/wiki/Higher_education" title="Higher education">higher education</a> by failing to expose students to the important products of Western civilization and thought.
</p><p>The essential component of such programs is a high degree of engagement with primary texts, called the Great Books. The curricula of Great Books programs often follow a canon of texts considered more or less essential to a student's education, such as Plato's <i>Republic</i>, or Dante's <i>Divine Comedy</i>. Such programs often focus exclusively on Western culture. Their employment of primary texts dictates an interdisciplinary approach, as most of the Great Books do not fall neatly under the prerogative of a single contemporary academic discipline. Great Books programs often include designated discussion groups as well as lectures, and have small class sizes. In general students in such programs receive an abnormally high degree of attention from their professors, as part of the overall aim of fostering a community of learning.
</p><p>Over 100 institutions of higher learning, mostly in the United States, offer some version of a Great Books Program as an option for students.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For much of the 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Library" title="Modern Library">Modern Library</a> provided a larger convenient list of the Western canon, i.e. those books  any person (or any English-speaking person) needed to know in order to claim an excellent general education.  The list numbered more than 300 items by the 1950s, by authors from Aristotle to Albert Camus, and has continued to grow. When in the 1990s the concept of the Western canon was vehemently condemned, just as earlier Modern Library lists had been criticized as "too American," Modern Library responded by preparing new lists of "100 Best Novels" and "100 Best Nonfiction" compiled by famous writers, and later compiled lists nominated by book purchasers and readers.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Debate">Debate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Debate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="Dead_white_men"></span>
</p><p>There has been an ongoing debate, especially in the US, over the nature and status of the canon since at least the 1960s, much of which is rooted in <a href="/wiki/Critical_theory" title="Critical theory">critical theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Critical_race_theory" title="Critical race theory">critical race theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In particular, <a href="/wiki/Postmodern" class="mw-redirect" title="Postmodern">postmodern</a> studies have argued that the body of scholarship is biased, because the main focus traditionally of the academic studies of history and <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> has only been on Europe and men. American philosopher <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jay_Stevenson&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jay Stevenson (page does not exist)">Jay Stevenson</a> argues:
</p>
<dl><dd>[In] the postmodern period […] [t]raditional literature has been found to have been written by "dead white males" to serve the <i>ideological</i> aims of a conservative and repressive Anglo <i>hegemony</i> […] In an array of reactions against the race, gender, and class biases found to be woven into the tradition of Anglo lit, multicultural writers and political literary theorists have sought to expose, resist, and redress injustices and prejudices.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Classicist" class="mw-redirect" title="Classicist">Classicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Knox" title="Bernard Knox">Bernard Knox</a> made direct reference to this topic when he delivered his 1992 <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Lecture" title="Jefferson Lecture">Jefferson Lecture</a> (the U.S. federal government's highest honor for achievement in the <a href="/wiki/Humanities" title="Humanities">humanities</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-jefflect_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jefflect-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>  Knox used the intentionally "provocative" title "The Oldest Dead White European Males"<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> as the title of his lecture and his subsequent book of the same name, in both of which Knox defended the continuing relevance of <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classical</a> culture to modern society.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lehmann_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lehmann-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some intellectuals have championed a "high conservative modernism" that insists that universal truths exist, and have opposed approaches that deny the existence of universal truths.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Many argued that "<a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>" was the repository of timeless truths.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allan_Bloom" title="Allan Bloom">Allan Bloom</a>, in his highly influential <i><a href="/wiki/The_Closing_of_the_American_Mind" title="The Closing of the American Mind">The Closing of the American Mind: How Higher Education Has Failed Democracy and Impoverished the Souls of Today's Students</a></i> (1987) argues that moral degradation results from ignorance of the great <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a> that shaped Western culture. Bloom further comments: "But one thing is certain: wherever the Great Books make up a central part of the curriculum, the students are excited and satisfied."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> His book was widely cited by some intellectuals for its argument that the classics contained universal truths and timeless values which were being ignored by <a href="/wiki/Cultural_relativism" title="Cultural relativism">cultural relativists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a> Professor of Humanities and famous literary critic <a href="/wiki/Harold_Bloom" title="Harold Bloom">Harold Bloom</a> (no relation) has also argued strongly in favor of the canon, in his 1994 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Western_Canon:_The_Books_and_School_of_the_Ages" title="The Western Canon: The Books and School of the Ages">The Western Canon: The Books and School of the Ages</a></i>,  and in general the canon remains as a represented idea in many institutions,<sup id="cite_ref-Searle_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Searle-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> though its implications continue to be debated.
</p><p>Defenders maintain that those who undermine the canon do so out of primarily political interests, and that such criticisms are misguided and/or disingenuous. As <a href="/wiki/John_Searle" title="John Searle">John Searle</a>, Professor Emeritus of Philosophy at the University of California, Berkeley, has written:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There is a certain irony in this [i.e., politicized objections to the canon] in that earlier student generations, my own for example, found the critical tradition that runs from <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> through the <a href="/wiki/The_Federalist_Papers" title="The Federalist Papers"><i>Federalist Papers</i></a>, through the writings of <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">Mill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx</a>, down to the twentieth century, to be liberating from the stuffy conventions of traditional American politics and pieties. Precisely by inculcating a critical attitude, the "canon" served to demythologize the conventional pieties of the American bourgeoisie and provided the student with a perspective from which to critically analyze American culture and institutions. Ironically, the same tradition is now regarded as oppressive. The texts once served an unmasking function; now we are told that it is the texts which must be unmasked.<sup id="cite_ref-Searle_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Searle-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>One of the main objections to a canon of literature is the question of authority; who should have the power to determine what works are worth reading? Searle's rebuttal suggests that "one obvious difficulty with it [i.e., arguments against hierarchical ranking of books] is that if it were valid, it would argue against any set of required readings whatever; indeed, any list you care to make about anything automatically creates two categories, those that are on the list and those that are not."<sup id="cite_ref-Searle_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Searle-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Altieri" title="Charles Altieri">Charles Altieri</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, states that canons are "an institutional form for exposing people to a range of idealized attitudes." It is according to this notion that work may be removed from the canon over time to reflect the contextual relevance and thoughts of society.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> American historian <a href="/wiki/Todd_M._Compton" class="mw-redirect" title="Todd M. Compton">Todd M. Compton</a> argues that canons are always communal in nature; that there are limited canons for, say a literature survey class, or an English department reading list, but there is no such thing as one absolute canon of literature. Instead, there are many conflicting canons. He regards Bloom's "Western Canon" as a personal canon only.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The process of defining the boundaries of the canon is endless. The philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Searle" title="John Searle">John Searle</a> has said, "In my experience there never was, in fact, a fixed 'canon'; there was rather a certain set of tentative judgments about what had importance and quality. Such judgments are always subject to revision, and in fact they were constantly being revised."<sup id="cite_ref-Searle_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Searle-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> One of the notable attempts at compiling an authoritative canon for literature in the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>-speaking world was the <i><a href="/wiki/Great_Books_of_the_Western_World" title="Great Books of the Western World">Great Books of the Western World</a></i> program. This program, developed in the middle third of the 20th century, grew out of the curriculum at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>. University president <a href="/wiki/Robert_Maynard_Hutchins" title="Robert Maynard Hutchins">Robert Maynard Hutchins</a> and his collaborator <a href="/wiki/Mortimer_Adler" class="mw-redirect" title="Mortimer Adler">Mortimer Adler</a> developed a program that offered reading lists, books, and organizational strategies for reading clubs to the general public.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> An earlier attempt had been made in 1909 by <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> president <a href="/wiki/Charles_W._Eliot" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles W. Eliot">Charles W. Eliot</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Classics" title="Harvard Classics">Harvard Classics</a>, a 51-volume anthology of classic works from world literature. Eliot's view was the same as that of Scottish philosopher and historian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a>: "The true University of these days is a Collection of Books". ("The Hero as Man of Letters", 1840)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_English-speaking_world">In the English-speaking world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: In the English-speaking world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="British_renaissance_poetry">British renaissance poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: British renaissance poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_literature" title="Elizabethan literature">Elizabethan literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_poets" title="Metaphysical poets">Metaphysical poets</a></div>
<p>The canon of Renaissance English poetry of the 16th and early 17th century has always been in some form of flux and towards the end of the 20th century the established canon was criticized, especially by those who wished to expand it to include, for example, more women writers.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> However, the central figures of the British renaissance canon remain, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sidney" title="Philip Sidney">Philip Sidney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">John Donne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Spenser</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">Donne</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Jonson</a> were major influences on 17th-century poetry. However, poet <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a> condemned aspects of the metaphysical poets in his criticism. In the 18th century <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Metaphysical poetry">Metaphysical poetry</a> fell into further disrepute,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> while the interest in <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_literature" title="Elizabethan literature">Elizabethan poetry</a> was rekindled through the scholarship of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Warton" title="Thomas Warton">Thomas Warton</a> and others. However, the canon of Renaissance poetry was formed in the Victorian period with anthologies like Palgrave's <i><a href="/wiki/Golden_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Treasury">Golden Treasury</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the twentieth century <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yvor_Winters" title="Yvor Winters">Yvor Winters</a> were two literary critics who were especially concerned with revising the canon of renaissance English literature. Eliot, for example, championed poet <a href="/wiki/Sir_John_Davies" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir John Davies">Sir John Davies</a> in an article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times_Literary_Supplement" title="The Times Literary Supplement">The Times Literary Supplement</a></i> in 1926. During the course of the 1920s, Eliot did much to establish the importance of the metaphysical school, both through his critical writing and by applying their method in his own work. However, by 1961 <a href="/wiki/A._Alvarez" class="mw-redirect" title="A. Alvarez">A. Alvarez</a> was commenting that "it may perhaps be a little late in the day to be writing about the Metaphysicals. The great vogue for Donne passed with the passing of the Anglo-American experimental movement in modern poetry."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Two decades later, a hostile view was expressed that emphasis on their importance had been an attempt by Eliot and his followers to impose a 'high Anglican and royalist literary history' on 17th-century English poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-ODNB_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODNB-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The American critic <a href="/wiki/Yvor_Winters" title="Yvor Winters">Yvor Winters</a> suggested in 1939 an alternative canon of <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan poetry">Elizabethan poetry</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> which would exclude the famous representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Petrarchan" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrarchan">Petrarchan school</a> of poetry, represented by Sir <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sidney" title="Philip Sidney">Philip Sidney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>. Winters claimed that the Native or Plain Style <i>anti-Petrarchan</i> movement had been undervalued and argued that <a href="/wiki/George_Gascoigne" title="George Gascoigne">George Gascoigne</a> (1525–1577) "deserves to be ranked […] among the six or seven greatest lyric poets of the century, and perhaps higher".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Towards the end of the 20th century the established canon was increasingly under fire.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_the_literary_canon_in_the_20th_century">Expansion of the literary canon in the 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Expansion of the literary canon in the 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the twentieth century there was a general reassessment of the <a href="/wiki/Literary_canon" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary canon">literary canon</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_writing_in_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Women&#39;s writing in English">women's writing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Post-colonial_literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-colonial literature">post-colonial literatures</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_literature" title="LGBT literature">gay and lesbian literature</a>, writing by people of colour, working people's writing, and the cultural productions of historically marginalized groups. This reassessment has resulted in a whole scale expansion of what is considered "literature", and genres hitherto not regarded as "literary", such as children's writing, journals, letters, travel writing, and many others are now the subjects of scholarly interest.<sup id="cite_ref-Blain_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blain-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Western literary canon has also expanded to include the literature of Asia, Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, and South America. Writers from Africa, Turkey, China, Egypt, Peru, and Colombia, Japan, etc., have received Nobel prizes since the late 1960s. Writers from Asia and Africa have also been nominated for, and also won, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_winners_and_shortlisted_authors_of_the_Booker_Prize_for_Fiction" class="mw-redirect" title="List of winners and shortlisted authors of the Booker Prize for Fiction">Booker prize</a> in recent years.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminism_and_the_literary_canon">Feminism and the literary canon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Feminism and the literary canon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/%C3%89criture_f%C3%A9minine" title="Écriture féminine">Écriture féminine</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_American_feminist_literature" title="List of American feminist literature">List of American feminist literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_feminist_literature" title="List of feminist literature">List of feminist literature</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_feminist_poets" title="List of feminist poets">List of feminist poets</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg/150px-Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg/225px-Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg/300px-Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1366" data-file-height="2127" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sartre_and_de_Beauvoir_at_Balzac_Memorial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simone_de_Beauvoir" title="Simone de Beauvoir">Simone de Beauvoir</a> at <a href="/wiki/Balzac" class="mw-redirect" title="Balzac">Balzac</a> Memorial</div></div></div>
<p>The feminist movement produced both feminist fiction and non-fiction and created new interest in women's writing. It also prompted a general reevaluation of women's <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_history" title="Women&#39;s history">historical</a> and academic contributions in response to the belief that women's lives and contributions have been underrepresented as areas of scholarly interest.<sup id="cite_ref-Blain_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blain-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, in Britain and America at least women achieved major literary success from the late eighteenth century, and many major nineteenth-century British novelists were women, including <a href="/wiki/Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen">Jane Austen</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bront%C3%ABs" class="mw-redirect" title="Brontës">Brontë family</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Gaskell" title="Elizabeth Gaskell">Elizabeth Gaskell</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Ann_Evans" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary Ann Evans">George Eliot</a>. There were also three major female poets, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Barrett_Browning" title="Elizabeth Barrett Browning">Elizabeth Barrett Browning</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Leighton_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leighton-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christina_Rossetti" title="Christina Rossetti">Christina Rossetti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emily_Dickinson" title="Emily Dickinson">Emily Dickinson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bloo9_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloo9-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> In the twentieth century there were also many major female writers, including <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Mansfield" title="Katherine Mansfield">Katherine Mansfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Richardson" title="Dorothy Richardson">Dorothy Richardson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eudora_Welty" title="Eudora Welty">Eudora Welty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Moore" title="Marianne Moore">Marianne Moore</a>. Notable female writers in France include <a href="/wiki/Colette" title="Colette">Colette</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Simone_de_Beauvoir" title="Simone de Beauvoir">Simone de Beauvoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_Yourcenar" title="Marguerite Yourcenar">Marguerite Yourcenar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nathalie_Sarraute" title="Nathalie Sarraute">Nathalie Sarraute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_Duras" title="Marguerite Duras">Marguerite Duras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Sagan" title="Françoise Sagan">Françoise Sagan</a>&#160;; and in Russia, <a href="/wiki/Anna_Akhmatova" title="Anna Akhmatova">Anna Akhmatova</a>.
</p><p>Much of the early period of feminist literary scholarship was given over to the rediscovery and reclamation of texts written by women. <a href="/wiki/Virago_Press" title="Virago Press">Virago Press</a> began to publish its large list of 19th and early 20th-century novels in 1975 and became one of the first commercial presses to join in the project of reclamation.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_authors">Black authors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Black authors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the twentieth century, the Western literary canon started to include black writers not only from <a href="/wiki/African-American_literature" title="African-American literature">black American writers</a>, but also from the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">wider black diaspora</a> of writers in Britain, France, Latin America, and Africa. This is largely due to the shift in social and political views during the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> in the United States.  The first global recognition came in 1950 when <a href="/wiki/Gwendolyn_Brooks" title="Gwendolyn Brooks">Gwendolyn Brooks</a> was the first black American to win a <a href="/wiki/Pulitzer_Prize" title="Pulitzer Prize">Pulitzer Prize</a> for Literature. <a href="/wiki/Chinua_Achebe" title="Chinua Achebe">Chinua Achebe</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Things_Fall_Apart" title="Things Fall Apart">Things Fall Apart</a></i> helped draw attention to <a href="/wiki/African_literature" title="African literature">African literature</a>. Nigerian <a href="/wiki/Wole_Soyinka" title="Wole Soyinka">Wole Soyinka</a> was the first African to win the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in Literature in 1986, and American <a href="/wiki/Toni_Morrison" title="Toni Morrison">Toni Morrison</a> was the first black woman to win in 1993.
</p><p>Some early American <a href="/wiki/Black_writers" class="mw-redirect" title="Black writers">Black writers</a> were inspired to defy ubiquitous racial prejudice by proving themselves equal to white American authors. As Henry Louis Gates, Jr., has said, "it is fair to describe the subtext of the history of black letters as this urge to refute the claim that because blacks had no written traditions they were bearers of an inferior culture."<sup id="cite_ref-Stryz_p140_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stryz_p140-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>African-American writers were also attempting to subvert the literary and power traditions of the United States. Some scholars assert that writing has traditionally been seen as "something defined by the dominant culture as a white male activity."<sup id="cite_ref-Stryz_p140_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stryz_p140-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> This means that, in American society, literary acceptance has traditionally been intimately tied in with the very power dynamics which perpetrated such evils as racial discrimination. By borrowing from and incorporating the non-written oral traditions and folk life of the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">African diaspora</a>, African-American literature broke "the mystique of connection between literary authority and <a href="/wiki/Patriarchal" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchal">patriarchal</a> power."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In producing their own literature, African Americans were able to establish their own literary traditions devoid of the white intellectual filter. This view of African-American literature as a tool in the struggle for Black political and cultural liberation has been stated for decades, most famously by <a href="/wiki/W._E._B._Du_Bois" title="W. E. B. Du Bois">W. E. B. Du Bois</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jhu_theory_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jhu_theory-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WoleSoyinka2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/WoleSoyinka2015.jpg/220px-WoleSoyinka2015.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/WoleSoyinka2015.jpg/330px-WoleSoyinka2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/WoleSoyinka2015.jpg/440px-WoleSoyinka2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1885" data-file-height="2513" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WoleSoyinka2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Wole_Soyinka" title="Wole Soyinka">Wole Soyinka</a> in 2015.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_and_Africa">Asia and Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Asia and Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since the 1960s the Western literary canon has been expanded to include writers from Asia, Africa, and the Middle East. This is reflected in the Nobel prizes awarded in literature.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata">Yasunari Kawabata</a> (1899 – 1972)<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">Japanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Novelist" title="Novelist">novelist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Short_story" title="Short story">short story</a> writer whose spare, lyrical, subtly-shaded prose works won him the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel Prize for Literature">Nobel Prize for Literature</a> in 1968, the first Japanese author to receive the award. His works have enjoyed broad international appeal and are still widely read.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Naguib_Mahfouz" title="Naguib Mahfouz">Naguib Mahfouz</a> (1911 &#8211; 2006) was an <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egyptian</a> writer who won the 1988 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel Prize for Literature">Nobel Prize for Literature</a>. He is regarded as one of the first contemporary writers of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_literature" title="Arabic literature">Arabic literature</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Tawfiq_el-Hakim" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawfiq el-Hakim">Tawfiq el-Hakim</a>, to explore themes of <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> He published 34 novels, over 350 short stories, dozens of movie scripts, and five plays over a 70-year career. Many of his works have been made into <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_cinema" class="mw-redirect" title="Egyptian cinema">Egyptian</a> and foreign films.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kenzabur%C5%8D_%C5%8Ce" title="Kenzaburō Ōe">Kenzaburō Ōe</a> (b. 1935) is a Japanese writer and a major figure in contemporary <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a>. His novels, short stories, and essays, strongly influenced by French and American literature and <a href="/wiki/Literary_theory" title="Literary theory">literary theory</a>, deal with political, social, and philosophical issues, including <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anticonformism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anticonformism">social non-conformism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Existentialism" title="Existentialism">existentialism</a>. Ōe was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> in 1994 for creating "an <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Imaginary world">imagined world</a>, where life and myth condense to form a disconcerting picture of the human predicament today".<sup id="cite_ref-shimbun2008_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shimbun2008-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guan_Moye" class="mw-redirect" title="Guan Moye">Guan Moye</a> (b. 1955), better known by the pen name "Mo Yan", is a Chinese novelist and short story writer.  Donald Morrison of the U.S. news magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">TIME</a></i> referred to him as "one of the most famous, oft-banned and widely <a href="/wiki/Copyright_infringement" title="Copyright infringement">pirated</a> of all <a href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_writers" title="List of Chinese writers">Chinese writers</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-Holding_Up_Half_The_Sky_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holding_Up_Half_The_Sky-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Leach" title="Jim Leach">Jim Leach</a> called him the Chinese answer to <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Franz Kafka</a> or <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Heller" title="Joseph Heller">Joseph Heller</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leach_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leach-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> He is best known to Western readers for his 1987 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Sorghum_Clan" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Sorghum Clan">Red Sorghum Clan</a></i>, of which the <i>Red Sorghum</i> and <i>Sorghum Wine</i> volumes were later adapted for the film <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Sorghum_(film)" title="Red Sorghum (film)">Red Sorghum</a></i>. In 2012, Mo was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> for his work as a writer "who with <a href="/wiki/Hallucinatory_realism" title="Hallucinatory realism">hallucinatory realism</a> merges folk tales, history and the contemporary".<sup id="cite_ref-Mo_Yan_får_Nobelpriset_i_litteratur_2012_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mo_Yan_får_Nobelpriset_i_litteratur_2012-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Orhan_Pamuk" title="Orhan Pamuk">Orhan Pamuk</a> (b. 1952) is a <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a> novelist, screenwriter, academic, and recipient of the 2006 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a>. One of Turkey's most prominent novelists,<sup id="cite_ref-Kinzer1998-12-15_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinzer1998-12-15-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> his work has sold over thirteen million books in sixty-three languages,<sup id="cite_ref-AutoEC-3_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoEC-3-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> making him the country's best-selling writer.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoEC-4_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoEC-4-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Pamuk is the author of novels including <i><a href="/wiki/The_White_Castle" title="The White Castle">The White Castle</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Book_(Pamuk_novel)" title="The Black Book (Pamuk novel)">The Black Book</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Life_(novel)" title="The New Life (novel)">The New Life</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/My_Name_Is_Red" title="My Name Is Red">My Name Is Red</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_(Pamuk_novel)" title="Snow (Pamuk novel)">Snow</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Museum_of_Innocence" title="The Museum of Innocence">The Museum of Innocence</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Strangeness_in_My_Mind" title="A Strangeness in My Mind">A Strangeness in My Mind</a></i>. He is the Robert Yik-Fong Tam Professor in the Humanities at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, where he teaches <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Comparative_literature" title="Comparative literature">comparative literature</a>.
Born in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Pamuk is the first Turkish Nobel laureate. He is also the recipient of numerous other literary awards. <i>My Name Is Red</i> won the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Prix_du_Meilleur_Livre_%C3%89tranger" title="Prix du Meilleur Livre Étranger">Prix du Meilleur Livre Étranger</a>, 2002 <a href="/wiki/Premio_Grinzane_Cavour" class="mw-redirect" title="Premio Grinzane Cavour">Premio Grinzane Cavour</a>, and 2003 <a href="/wiki/International_Dublin_Literary_Award" title="International Dublin Literary Award">International Dublin Literary Award</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_canon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Octavio_Paz_Lozano" class="mw-redirect" title="Octavio Paz Lozano">Octavio Paz Lozano</a> (1914&#160;– 1998) was a Mexican poet and diplomat. For his body of work, he was awarded the 1981 <a href="/wiki/Miguel_de_Cervantes_Prize" title="Miguel de Cervantes Prize">Miguel de Cervantes Prize</a>, the 1982 <a href="/wiki/Neustadt_International_Prize_for_Literature" title="Neustadt International Prize for Literature">Neustadt International Prize for Literature</a>, and the 1990 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:135px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gabogarciamarquez1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Gabogarciamarquez1.png" decoding="async" width="133" height="207" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="133" data-file-height="207" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gabogarciamarquez1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Garc%C3%ADa_M%C3%A1rquez" class="mw-redirect" title="García Márquez">García Márquez</a> signing a copy of <i><a href="/wiki/One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, Cuba</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Garc%C3%ADa_M%C3%A1rquez" title="Gabriel García Márquez">Gabriel García Márquez</a><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>  (1927 – 2014) was a <a href="/wiki/Colombian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Colombian people">Colombian</a> novelist, short-story writer, screenwriter, and journalist. Considered one of the most significant authors of the 20th century and one of the best in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_literature" title="Spanish literature">Spanish language</a>, he was awarded the 1972 <a href="/wiki/Neustadt_International_Prize_for_Literature" title="Neustadt International Prize for Literature">Neustadt International Prize for Literature</a> and the 1982 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
